  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_7e69fe4f-c582-43ca-8aa5-86ccc5f9e996'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13865135004595452920">Kundera initially intended to call this novel <em>The Lyrical Age</em>. The lyrical age, according to Kundera, is youth, and this novel, above all, is an epic of adolescence; an ironic epic that tenderly erodes sacrosanct values: childhood, motherhood, revolution, and even poetry. Jaromil is in fact a poet. His mother made him a poet and accompanies him (figuratively) to his love b</span>
  <span id="freeText13865135004595452920" style="display:none">Kundera initially intended to call this novel <em>The Lyrical Age</em>. The lyrical age, according to Kundera, is youth, and this novel, above all, is an epic of adolescence; an ironic epic that tenderly erodes sacrosanct values: childhood, motherhood, revolution, and even poetry. Jaromil is in fact a poet. His mother made him a poet and accompanies him (figuratively) to his love bed, and (literally) to his deathbed. A ridiculous and touching character, horrifying and totally innocent ("innocence with its bloody smile"!), Jaromil is at the same time a true poet. He's no creep, he's Rimbaud. Rimbaud entrapped by the communist revolution, entrapped in a sombre farce.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13865135004595452920" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

        $('edition_268449').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_268449').hasClassName('ignored')) {

</div>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/62694548-rasool-ghanbari"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1482241024i/62694548._UX100_CR0,18,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/62694548-rasool-ghanbari">Rasool Ghanbari</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">جمهوری چک میلان کوندرا: زندگی جای دیگری است<br/>شاید علت اساسی همین است که میلان کوندرا می‌گوید، همه مثل همین تلف کردن ساعت‌های عمر باشند ، همیشه به بهانه <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">جمهوری چک میلان کوندرا: زندگی جای دیگری است<br />شاید علت اساسی همین است که میلان کوندرا می‌گوید، همه مثل همین تلف کردن ساعت‌های عمر باشند ، همیشه به بهانه زندگی کردن آن‌هم درجای که واقعاً باورش کمی سختِ .فکرمی کنیم این همان‌جاست یا باید جای دیگری باشد ؟ بز رک‌ترین مشکل در زندگی یک‌جور فهمیدن است ،آن را عوضی جای چیزی می‌گیریم که نمی‌فهمی کدام درست است . درک کردن ، فهمیدن یک فرم اساسی در زندگی ما است ، همیشه هم درهمیم جا اشتباهی می‌کنیم ، باید درست فهمید آن‌هم نه در جای عوضی . میلان کوندرا زندگی جای دیگری است پیروی از زندگی یارو میل شاعر متوسط در حال رشد بین جنگ جهانی دوم و بهار پراگ. حاصل یک ازدواج عشق و یک بدترکیبی در رابطه یک زوج که از همان ابتدا تمایل برکنار هم ندارند، باری ناخواسته برای پدر، و تمنای مادر برای کودک که زندگی خود را به شاعر جوان اختصاص می‌دهد رابطه‌ای حیرانی و شگفت‌زده از هما ابتدا که اتاق خاص او را می‌آراید وکوندرا به‌درستی گم‌شدن هم مادر و شاعر جوان را در لایه‌های زندگی به‌خوبی نشان می‌دهد وقتی درباره بدن زن حامله و کودک او قلم‌فرسائی می‌کند موجی ارز گمان زدن ایجادمی کند هیچ جسمی صمیمی‌تر از بدن انسان به خودش نیست به‌درستی رشد را و نزدیکی آن به (خود) را در اندیشه انسان نشان می‌دهد موضوع اساسی در فهمی است که آن‌ها از خود نشان می‌دهند ، در ابتدای رمان ماجرای لقاح دختر باکره وان چه اندیشه را به مادر باکره می‌کشاند و تصوری که از آپولو ارائه می‌دهد ، یارو میل رشد می‌کند تا خراب شود ویران گردد ، سرگردانی و پریشانی او در رشد حزب کمونیست در چک و لو دادن دوست‌دخترش را به دست جلاد ، و یک رابط ویران‌کننده مادر و فرزند رمانی خلق‌شده است که بشدت تکان‌دهنده است . تأسف برای زندگی مادری در پایان داستان دیگری اثری از آن نیست اما ، تصور می‌شود که بیهودگی تنها نتیجه آن است . بیهودگی که شاید در پایان اکثر قصه‌های ما نیز باشد . مادری که خود را، که، داشتن عشق و ازدواج ، زندگی خود را ، همه‌چیز. اختصاص برای او .به یارو میل<br /><a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_2134273493" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2134273493" />
    <a title="Mutasim Billah " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20438238-mutasim-billah"><img alt="Mutasim Billah " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536682441p2/20438238.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2134273493?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mutasim Billah " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mutasim Billah " href="/user/show/20438238-mutasim-billah">Mutasim Billah </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571149032" href="/book/show/1242411.Life_Is_Elsewhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20438238-mutasim-billah?shelf=czech">czech</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2134273493" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8615996649873818555"><i>“You think that just because it's already happened, the past is finished and unchangeable? Oh no, the past is cloaked in multicolored taffeta and every time we look at it we see a different hue.” </i><br/><br/><i>Life is Elsewhere</i> is Kundera's parody of youth and adolescence. It ridicules the ego of young artists and makes a folly out of sanctified values of the time: motherhood, poetry, revolution, nationalism. Don't get me wrong: Kundera will never sound that harsh, he puts forward his satire with tenderness m</span>
  <span id="freeText8615996649873818555" style="display:none"><i>“You think that just because it's already happened, the past is finished and unchangeable? Oh no, the past is cloaked in multicolored taffeta and every time we look at it we see a different hue.” </i><br><br><i>Life is Elsewhere</i> is Kundera's parody of youth and adolescence. It ridicules the ego of young artists and makes a folly out of sanctified values of the time: motherhood, poetry, revolution, nationalism. Don't get me wrong: Kundera will never sound that harsh, he puts forward his satire with tenderness mixed with allegory and charm. His wordplay is sublime and the plot is built with an architect's precision. <br><br>The story is told from a third-person narrative, in seven parts. Our hero, Jaromil is a young <i>Don Quixote</i> in Czechoslovakia with a passion for poetry. The story starts way before that, though. It begins from his mother's youth, from the conception of Jaromil. The story explores poets and their eccentric rites and inspirations, their unusual oedipal bonds. There are fables and stories based on real-life poets (Arthur Rimbaud, Mikhail Lermontov) woven into the plot. The book also explores <i>damnatio memoriae</i> during the Communist revolution and blacklisting of artists and lyricists who dabbled in surrealism.<br><br>All the typical gems of the Kundera style are present here, though the novel's story has a slow start. The book has a more-or-less linear story-line.<br><br>A wonderful reading experience.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1530119979i/25867686.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="8615996649873818555" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3328456766" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3328456766" />
    <a title="Kevin Ansbro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43579369-kevin-ansbro"><img alt="Kevin Ansbro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506848923p2/43579369.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3328456766?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin Ansbro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin Ansbro" href="/user/show/43579369-kevin-ansbro">Kevin Ansbro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571149032" href="/book/show/1242411.Life_Is_Elsewhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=classic-literature">classic-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=human-emotions">human-emotions</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=gentle-humour">gentle-humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43579369-kevin-ansbro?shelf=literature-for-grown-ups">literature-for-grown-ups</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3328456766" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7828208095902152580"><i>
  <b>"Mother love stamps the foreheads of boys with a stigma that repels the friendship of buddies."</b>
</i><br/>—Milan Kundera, Life is Elsewhere<br/><br/>From the day he was born, Czechoslovakian baby, Jaromil, is spoon-fed poetry and spoilt rotten by his coddling mother. So it's no surprise that the boy becomes brattish and ostentatious, incurring the enmity of his peers. And each time it rained, his mother would wait for him at the school gates with a big umbrella, while his schoolmates waded through puddles, their</span>
  <span id="freeText7828208095902152580" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>"Mother love stamps the foreheads of boys with a stigma that repels the friendship of buddies."</b>
</i><br />—Milan Kundera, Life is Elsewhere<br /><br />From the day he was born, Czechoslovakian baby, Jaromil, is spoon-fed poetry and spoilt rotten by his coddling mother. So it's no surprise that the boy becomes brattish and ostentatious, incurring the enmity of his peers. And each time it rained, his mother would wait for him at the school gates with a big umbrella, while his schoolmates waded through puddles, their shoes slung over shoulders. <br /><br />Into adulthood, our vainglorious poet maintains his overblown sense of importance, imagining himself an artist of greater eminence than he actually is. As Wharton would say, he is the reflection of a candle in a mirror, rather than the candle itself. <br />One senses that Milan Kundera was poking fun at some (or just one) of his literary <i>confrères</i>. I imagined he was getting something off his chest, annoyed that lesser-talented writers were getting all the praise.<br /><br />It is said that Kundera has done for his native Czechoslovakia what Gabriel García Márquez did for Latin America. Well, that might be so; he does have an allegorical writing style and also shares Márquez's fondness for the absurd but, for me, he lacks the lyrical wordplay of the Colombian. <br />I dimly remember my wife enjoying Kundera's <i>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</i> many years ago, so she's definitely a fan. <br /><br />The book was really good, but not really great. It didn't grab me by the lapels and kiss me. I was frustrated by the author's restraint – presumably instilled in him during a childhood of communist oppression. <br />I did, however, like Kundera's pithy observations, his irreverence and his understated humour.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7828208095902152580" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1754669490" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1754669490" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1754669490?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible-uk">audible-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=returned">returned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=czech-republic">czech-republic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=philo-psychol">philo-psychol</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2016-read">2016-read</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1754669490" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11164840966247328832">I finished this book wondering if I had understood correctly what the author was trying to say. I have all sorts of ideas, but they don't hold together into one cohesive message. If I don’t understand the book, how can I give it more stars? <br/><br/>The pluses are that the book keeps you thinking, it has sentences that cleverly hint at philosophical messages and lots of amusing lines. The humor is satirical irony. <br/><br/>The sentence in the GR book review stating that this novel is, “<i>an ironic story <s>epic</s> that h</i></span>
  <span id="freeText11164840966247328832" style="display:none">I finished this book wondering if I had understood correctly what the author was trying to say. I have all sorts of ideas, but they don't hold together into one cohesive message. If I don’t understand the book, how can I give it more stars? <br /><br />The pluses are that the book keeps you thinking, it has sentences that cleverly hint at philosophical messages and lots of amusing lines. The humor is satirical irony. <br /><br />The sentence in the GR book review stating that this novel is, “<i>an ironic story <s>epic</s> that humorously <s>tenderly</s> erodes sacrosanct values: childhood, motherhood, revolution, and even poetry</i>” is an almost perfect description of this book! I have replaced the word “epic” with “story” and “tenderly” with “humorously”. Little is sacrosanct in this book. Not politics. Certainly not sex. Kundera’s books always contain a heavy dose of eroticism. <br /><br />So how does the story unroll and what does the book deal with? First and foremost, the relationship between an adolescent son and his doting mother. He is tied to his mother's <b>very</b> short apron strings. I cannot imagine any reader liking any of the characters. The plot jumps around; it is meant to confound; it is meant to be confusing. It is meant to keep you thinking. It is not the steps of the story we are to follow but rather the underlying philosophical messages we are meant to think about. The author himself interrupts the events and speaks directly to the readers explaining why he has chosen to flip to another episode. The setting is Prague at the end of the forties and early fifties.<br /><br />Maybe we are not supposed to draw any deep conclusion. Maybe we are simply to laugh. Laugh at society? Laugh at ourselves? What I kept thinking about was how the son never said anything original; he spoke only clever lines that someone else had said or expressed views that one should say. When what one should say changed, what he said changed too. <br /><br />The book was written by the author in 1969 in Czech. Then it was translated into French. In 1985 the French translation was revised by the author in an attempt to better correspond to the original. An English translation from the revised French translation was done by Aaron Asher. This was done in close cooperation with the author to insure that no new distortion should occur.<br /><br />I enjoyed the audiobook narration by Richmond Roxie. <br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11164840966247328832" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_31909566" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31909566" />
    <a title="Eszter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72271-eszter"><img alt="Eszter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1178202646p2/72271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/31909566?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eszter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eszter" href="/user/show/72271-eszter">Eszter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31909566" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8884177962160343341">the first hundred pages or so made me really anxious because i just couldn't make myself like the book, and who am i, really, if i am actively disliking a book by kundera?? i was like, yeah, uh huh, i get what you're doing with the misogyny, but please, either knock it off or redeem your little monster of a protagonist, stat. and why are there still two hundred more pages left? what. a. chump. (though i was hoping he was just toying with me.)<br/><br/>then! before i knew what was happening, kundera zipped</span>
  <span id="freeText8884177962160343341" style="display:none">the first hundred pages or so made me really anxious because i just couldn't make myself like the book, and who am i, really, if i am actively disliking a book by kundera?? i was like, yeah, uh huh, i get what you're doing with the misogyny, but please, either knock it off or redeem your little monster of a protagonist, stat. and why are there still two hundred more pages left? what. a. chump. (though i was hoping he was just toying with me.)<br /><br />then! before i knew what was happening, kundera zipped past me and i was thrown around by wild waves of sympathy/antipathy, victory/desolation and all sorts of other strong, conflicting emotions. this book is at once a powerful kick in the gut to sentimentality and a celebration of the realm of youth and feeling; a dizzyingly painful portrait of socialism and a congratulatory handshake to those who will fight for what they believe in; a parody starring poets of revolutionary eras and a lasting monument to them. on the one hand, the plot is fantastically simple to predict, and on the other, you begin to doubt your predictions as his treatment of the main character gets more and more brutal. but then, when is kundera's focus ever really on the plot? and when is his brutality not at the same time tenderness? and how can i give anything less than three stars to a book that takes a few pages to be meta and talk about how the book is framing the protagonist's existence? <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8884177962160343341" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_728377723" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/728377723" />
    <a title="Joané Gous" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5746678-joan-gous"><img alt="Joané Gous" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582482310p2/5746678.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/728377723?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joané Gous" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joané Gous" href="/user/show/5746678-joan-gous">Joané Gous</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571197774" href="/book/show/678961.Life_is_Elsewhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746678-joan-gous?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746678-joan-gous?shelf=postmodernist">postmodernist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746678-joan-gous?shelf=literary">literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746678-joan-gous?shelf=czech">czech</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer728377723" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10663142786724133046">I don't know what it is exactly that makes me want to write my first ever review on Goodreads after reading this novel, and yet, after reading it, I know it is impossible to simply let it go. So here goes...<br/><br/>Every time I pick up a Kundera novel I'm certain that I am either going to love or hate the novel. This one, within the first few pages, convinced me that I was going to hate it. After forty pages I despised it. The lead character is a self-centered sorry excuse of a human being, who has no i</span>
  <span id="freeText10663142786724133046" style="display:none">I don't know what it is exactly that makes me want to write my first ever review on Goodreads after reading this novel, and yet, after reading it, I know it is impossible to simply let it go. So here goes...<br /><br />Every time I pick up a Kundera novel I'm certain that I am either going to love or hate the novel. This one, within the first few pages, convinced me that I was going to hate it. After forty pages I despised it. The lead character is a self-centered sorry excuse of a human being, who has no identity what-so-ever to call his own. The first forty pages convinced me that I hated the protagonist. And then... The moment I embraced the fact that I hated the novel, that I despised its characters I couldn't put it down. Suddenly the novel opened up to me and revealed to me its inner workings. <br /><br />Jaromil (without giving too much away I hope) is what exactly? Is he the product, the victim, of his circumstances? A boy raised by an over-protective, maniacal mother, who tries to absorb her own son into her very being, refusing to acknowledge that he is a person on his own? Or is he, rather, despicable because of his weakness and ignorance to take control of his own life? He has a warped sense of what it means to be a man, every time he makes a statement he simply regurgitates the ideas of others and passes it on as his own. Jaromil is a product of society, never once adopting an individual personality. He becomes his mother, his mentor (the painter), the revolutionary and yet, not once is there any sense of Jaromil himself. His life is meaningless, and all his actions are meaningless and in the end his death, too, is meaningless. <br /><br />What makes this novel so powerful, however, is the fact that it rings so close to the truth. So close to what we, in this society we deem to call modern, have become. There's no space for the individual anymore, and Kundera sums it up perfectly when he writes: "The worst thing isn't that the world isn't free, but that people have unlearned their freedom" We are the products of the societies we function in, no space for individualism, yet we feed society by relinquishing to it our right to freedom, our right to express ourselves as individuals and our right to live our lives the way we want to live them. Borders (both literal and figurative) define who we are and will define generations to come...<br /><br />This novel touched me in ways I never expected it would and because of its nihilistic ideals, because of the way it forced me to inspect my own life and the paths I've chosen every time I reached a crossroad, I rate this novel five stars instead of one. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10663142786724133046" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1968876629" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1968876629" />
    <a title="Greg Brozeit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26552534-greg-brozeit"><img alt="Greg Brozeit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544324486p2/26552534.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1968876629?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg Brozeit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg Brozeit" href="/user/show/26552534-greg-brozeit">Greg Brozeit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140064704" href="/book/show/920141.Life_Is_Elsewhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26552534-greg-brozeit?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1968876629" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8084915364857610893">This year is the fiftieth since Kundera finished writing <i>Life is Elsewhere</i> (publication came a few years later). Although the plot is connected to an era of Czech history, I couldn’t help but impose my own experiences and contemporary events to my reading of it. That is the mark of great literature; it steps outside of its obvious confines as it emerges to illuminate universal and eternal questions. Don’t be fooled by the simple prose of this hypnotizing novel, its observations are percipient in</span>
  <span id="freeText8084915364857610893" style="display:none">This year is the fiftieth since Kundera finished writing <i>Life is Elsewhere</i> (publication came a few years later). Although the plot is connected to an era of Czech history, I couldn’t help but impose my own experiences and contemporary events to my reading of it. That is the mark of great literature; it steps outside of its obvious confines as it emerges to illuminate universal and eternal questions. Don’t be fooled by the simple prose of this hypnotizing novel, its observations are percipient in ways that don’t register immediately, but they linger nonetheless. This is literature.<br /><br />Much like the little I have read of Kundera, the straightforward prose and allusions are a complex of layers that have to be peeled away with great attention. In this story we meet Jaromil, a mama’s boy who pretentiously aspires to be a great poet, something he never attains or deserves. He never learns that his “art” cannot exist independently in the duplicitous history of the early Czech Soviet Socialist Republic, which makes his self-centered actions even more consequential than he is capable of seeing. A great poet must have insight, a quality he never attains.<br /><br />Kundera’s interweaving of references of great poets from of revolutionary epochs ranging from the age of the great poets of the early 19th Century to the Paris student protests of 1968 only emphasize the shallowness of Jaromil’s life and delusions. Instead of living an authentic life of awareness, Jaromil’s is one of a search of revolutionary conformity—he never understands that those concepts cannot coexist. Instead he creates an altar ego, one who lives and acts in preposterous dream worlds with little bearing on reality. And when he is unable to distinguish between his dreams and real life, the truth ends up having profound consequences, on others as well as himself. Indeed, in a poignant conclusion to the novel, his dream world abandons him, choosing truth over his contrived lies.<br /><br />The title of the novel comes from a verse in a Shelley poem. For Jaromil, life is truly elsewhere, but he never seems to know where or what it is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8084915364857610893" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_230762182" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/230762182" />
    <a title="Sarah Capps" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4044227-sarah-capps"><img alt="Sarah Capps" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481572411p2/4044227.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/230762182?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah Capps" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah Capps" href="/user/show/4044227-sarah-capps">Sarah Capps</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer230762182" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15005871566760652516"> “Because real life is elsewhere. The students are tearing up the cobblestones, overturning cars, building barricades; their irruption into the world is beautiful and noisy, illuminated by flames and greeted by explosions of tear-gas grenades. How much more painful was the lot of Rimbaud, who dreamed about the barricades of the Paris Commune and never got to it from Charleville. But in 1968 thousands of Rimbauds have their own barricades, behind which they stand and refuse any compromise with th</span>
  <span id="freeText15005871566760652516" style="display:none"> “Because real life is elsewhere. The students are tearing up the cobblestones, overturning cars, building barricades; their irruption into the world is beautiful and noisy, illuminated by flames and greeted by explosions of tear-gas grenades. How much more painful was the lot of Rimbaud, who dreamed about the barricades of the Paris Commune and never got to it from Charleville. But in 1968 thousands of Rimbauds have their own barricades, behind which they stand and refuse any compromise with the former masters of the world. The emancipation of mankind will be total, or it will not exist.<br /><br /> But only a kilometer from there, on the other bank of the Seine, the former masters of the world continue to live their lives, and the din of the Latin Quarter reaches them as something far away. Dream is reality, the students wrote on the walls, but it seems that the opposite was true: that reality (the barricades, the trees cut down, the red flags) was a dream.” Life is Elsewhere (pg 237)<br /><br />Life is Elsewhere by Milan Kundera is the ultimate attack on Romanticism—childhood, motherhood, love, revolution, and especially poetry. Published in 1973, the book takes place in Czechloslovakia before, during, and after the Second World War and focuses on a young poet and revolutionary named Jaromil. Because Jaromil’s father never wanted a family, the boy’s mother makes her son the center of her world. Nothing Jaromil does as a child goes unnoticed. Childishly cryptic remarks are seen as grand philosophical statements. The sketching of dog heads on human bodies due to the inability to draw human ones is seen as profound artistic vision. Simple rhymes are seen as poetic genius. As Jaromil grows up, he believes himself to be a prodigy, because he has always been told he is.<br /><br />However, as he gets older he begins to yearn for independence. He joins the revolution, tries to meet girls, becomes secretive, and attempts to turn himself into an individual by rejecting the philosophies of childhood mentors and taking opposing stances. The tragic story unfolds as time and time again the poet tries to free himself of his mother and finds the task an impossibility. Throughout the novel the reader’s feelings toward Jaromil are tugged from one extreme to the other. At times he is cruel, unforgivable, whiney, and concieted, and at others he’s a sympathetic victim of a clingy and embarrassing mother who will stop at nothing to make sure she’s the only woman in her son’s life.<br /><br />Kundera’s story is compelling, philosophical, brilliantly written, and filled with sadness as the the author no doubt thought back to his country before his exile.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15005871566760652516" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2831661531" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2831661531" />
    <a title="Sohaib" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31777090-sohaib"><img alt="Sohaib" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599511226p2/31777090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2831661531?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sohaib" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sohaib" href="/user/show/31777090-sohaib">Sohaib</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394480107" href="/book/show/1410790.Life_is_Elsewhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31777090-sohaib?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31777090-sohaib?shelf=2019-reads">2019-reads</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2831661531" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9870272943157902967"><b>"This novel is like you. It too would like to be other novels, those it might have been."</b><br/><br/>My third book for Kundera after <i>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</i> and <i>Identity</i>, and again, he doesn't disappoint. <br/><br/>Kundera keeps the authorial voice of a third-person omniscient narrator, guiding (perhaps controlling) our perception and understanding of the events as they unfold. The quotation above is an example, slightly though with a tinge of arrogance in the assumption that he (Mr. Narrator) knows us (the</span>
  <span id="freeText9870272943157902967" style="display:none"><b>"This novel is like you. It too would like to be other novels, those it might have been."</b><br /><br />My third book for Kundera after <i>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</i> and <i>Identity</i>, and again, he doesn't disappoint. <br /><br />Kundera keeps the authorial voice of a third-person omniscient narrator, guiding (perhaps controlling) our perception and understanding of the events as they unfold. The quotation above is an example, slightly though with a tinge of arrogance in the assumption that he (Mr. Narrator) knows us (the readers).<br /><br />For some reason, Kundera likes this idea of power—keeping power over his texts through third-person distant and philosophical narrators.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>Anyway...</b>
</i><br /><br />The books tells the story of a young poet who finds himself struggling with an absent father and an overbearing mother, unbeknownst to her of course since everything she does is hidden by the veil of Motherly Love. Jaromil, the young poet, struggles with his mother as she tries to stay close to him even though he seeks some healthy distance by dating another girl.<br /><br />The story encompasses Jaromil's entire life but this part, with its many ramifications, represents the crux of the narrative.<br /><br />Recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9870272943157902967" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_930497763" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/930497763" />
    <a title="Lucie Novak" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29972501-lucie-novak"><img alt="Lucie Novak" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1399465329p2/29972501.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

It is complicated. People who never lived under a totalitarian regime do not understand the complexity.<br />But the poet in this book betrays his friends, too, under pressure. Well written look in the past. I read the book as a samizdat- typed on shewets of paper, passed between friends, in danger of getting into great trouble if found. You can read it in the comfort of knowing you are not in danger. But who knows, that might make it less fun to read.</span>

<div id="review_696208226" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/696208226" />
    <a title="Tej" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5074564-tej"><img alt="Tej" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1460673152p2/5074564.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/696208226?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tej" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tej" href="/user/show/5074564-tej">Tej</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer696208226" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4382070065094743322">‘Life is elsewhere’ is about what?<br/> <br/>The question albeit futile, is pertinent from the exigency of not finding a well-knit congruent conglomeration of all the individually worthwhile ideas, pieces of brilliance that overtly or even covertly (raked my neural synaptic exchanges to discover) do not congeal into brilliant splendor which for me pulls this work down quite decidedly. I picked it up on whim based on the title and the descriptive excerpt although the greatest backing was the ‘Unbearable Li</span>
  <span id="freeText4382070065094743322" style="display:none">‘Life is elsewhere’ is about what?<br /> <br />The question albeit futile, is pertinent from the exigency of not finding a well-knit congruent conglomeration of all the individually worthwhile ideas, pieces of brilliance that overtly or even covertly (raked my neural synaptic exchanges to discover) do not congeal into brilliant splendor which for me pulls this work down quite decidedly. I picked it up on whim based on the title and the descriptive excerpt although the greatest backing was the ‘Unbearable Lightness of Being’ which enthralled me, enraptured me as one of the best among all I have read. My towering expectations brought me to the verge of ridiculing this one in the heat of unfulfilled prejudice but on cooling it down and envisioning through the eyes of open minded reverie, I find it to be good book that enriched me a trifle, but sadly, only that. The potentially gut wrenching experience that seemed round the corner always remained there, round the corner, never visited the gut. It must be read when one has eons of patience and no or little expectation or (harshly speaking) when lacking anything else ready to read (only my opinion of course.<br /><br />There is Jaromil and his mama around whom the story starts to weave itself which is sheer poetry at times when the existential chords of the two of them are explored. <br /><br /><i>The poignant end of rebellions! First she rebelled against her parents for the sake of the young engineer, and then she ran to her parents for help against him.</i><br /><br /><i>Wasn't her great love for the poet's father a romantic rebellion against the dullness and regularity of her parents' life? Wasn't there a hidden likeness between the untamed landscape and the boldness she, the daughter of a rich merchant, showed in choosing a penniless engineer who had just finished his studies?</i><br /><br /><i>This thought provided her a reassuring excuse, for it follows that she was brought into adultery not by her sensuality but by her innocence; and the thought of innocence immediately increased her anger toward the one who perpetually kept her in a state of innocent half maturity, and this anger fell like an iron curtain in front of her thoughts so that she only heard her breath quicken and she gave up pondering what she was doing.</i><br /><br />Jaromil, the proverbial ‘mama’s boy’ is portrayed as a prodigal son mainly on the force of backing bestowed upon him by his mama till he comes on his own and becomes a real poet but does he really? <br /><br /><i>in paradise there is no distinction between beauty and ugliness, so that all the things the body is made of were neither ugly nor beautiful but only delightful; even though toothless, the gums were delightful, the breast was delightful, the navel was delightful, the little bottom was delightful, the intestines—whose performance was closely overseen— were delightful, the standing hairs on the grotesque skull were delightful. She watched over her son's burps, pees, and poops not only with concern for the child's health; no, she watched over all the small body's activities with passion.</i><br /><br />The emotional insecurity and possessiveness of his mother gets complemented by the overt sensitivity and introvertedness of the son who at times is willing to leave all his poetry for a semblance of manliness only to recede behind the same lyricism for comfort as well as to find his own voice in the world that mauls him each time he ventures... poetry simply enables him to make ‘his own’ sense of it all. <br /><br /><i>But there was something more precious than his poems; something far away he didn't yet possess and longed for—manliness; he knew that it could only be attained by action and courage; and if courage meant courage to be rejected, rejected by everything, by the beloved woman, by the painter, and even by his own poems—so be it: he wanted to have that courage.</i><br /><br />It is this section of the book where the story is at its crescendo and strikes almost every chord of ‘Being Human’, while projecting a ‘coming to terms’ struggle of Jaromil with his adolescent physicality as well as emotional turmoil, in a growing artist of supposedly prodigal potentialities. He weaves a world of dreams, of dreams within dreams, where he almost ‘quixotically’ imagines all that he may have wished to do and potentially attain in real life. This world of his becomes his immunity, a cocoon that is ephemeral but becomes his eternal carrier in the worldly confusions he fails to grapple with.<br /><br /><i>The world was constantly wounding him; he blushed when he faced women, he was ashamed, and he saw ridicule everywhere. In his dreams of death he found silence; one could live there slowly, mutely, and happily. Yes, death, as Jaromil imagined it, was a lived death: it was oddly like that period when a person has no need to enter the world because he is a world unto himself....</i><br /><br />Jaromil’s dependencies upon his mama, his wishing to run away from her, at the same time needing her big time and she even reaching his love-bed in a fit of possessiveness is both comical and innocent and bodes well for the story. <br /><br />When it starts to dabble with politics, questions of art, life and philosophy, does the book starts hitting the nadir. Although well written and structured, these sections do not back themselves sufficiently to elicit belief or provide confidence that they are integral components of otherwise poignant story. Some of the most novel and intriguing ideas that author envisages about origins of poetic tendencies in a human in the context of Jaromil, of course, Jaromil’s prodigious talent or his mere desire to hide behind a veneer that comes across as poetry, comment on art via another character ‘a painter’ all are good writing but they are like skimmed portion when the milk is centrifuged – immiscible… the casein is missing, unfortunately. <br /><br />It all borders very nearly on being an exercise in futility.<br /><br />None of Kundera’s signature writing and imagery is missing, plots within plots wrought in non-linearity and individually these are bordering brilliance, raising the hope every time of witnessing a comprehensive towering piece of literature but right on the verge of potential orgasmic heights, one meets precipitous drabness repeatedly, salvaged only by the author’s appearance and sort of epilogues, couple of chapters at the end. The semblance of perspective is attained but fails to make an enrapturing sense of it all to sing paeans or vouch for it. I couldn’t.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4382070065094743322" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1946452812" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6042754279022906523">Shrieking, hard, irritating, and intimidating, fit only for belief revision, here is the work as an invitation for reconsideration, not merely a novel. Life is Elsewhere dislocates its readers and gets us out of the comfort zone, bringing us face to face with our worst fears. What if our lives did not really matter and what if our emotions were merely pathetic!<br/></span>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3437638536" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3437638536" />
    <a title="Kritanya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50382434-kritanya"><img alt="Kritanya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590445968p2/50382434.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3437638536?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kritanya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kritanya" href="/user/show/50382434-kritanya">Kritanya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3437638536" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12938921070997803586">It's not easy to truly describe the experience of reading a book by Milan Kundera. His prose is elusive and leaves you with the feeling like you're floating on nothing. I'm not sure how to even classify his writing style because I haven't read a lot of similar authors - I would say poetic, satirical and allegorical, or perhaps a combination of it. It starts out with one thing and ends in some kind of a profound and yet completely ordinary realization. I guess that's what I like about his books.<br/><br/>L</span>
  <span id="freeText12938921070997803586" style="display:none">It's not easy to truly describe the experience of reading a book by Milan Kundera. His prose is elusive and leaves you with the feeling like you're floating on nothing. I'm not sure how to even classify his writing style because I haven't read a lot of similar authors - I would say poetic, satirical and allegorical, or perhaps a combination of it. It starts out with one thing and ends in some kind of a profound and yet completely ordinary realization. I guess that's what I like about his books.<br /><br />Life is elsewhere is about a boy coddled by his mother and how that shapes their lives or doesn't. It's about the constant state of arousal and misfiring that is linked with growing up. And the consequences of emulating beliefs we don't quite understand. <br /><br />Kundera's defiance to stick to a plot or some kind of a structure is what keeps me wondering how he does what he does. He creates characters so true to themselves that you can't help but despise and appreciate their honesty and humaneness at the same time. His non-linear style can get pretty annoying but I didn't mind considering how it ended. <br /><br />To make one ruminate or introspect while brimming with ambiguity is what I consider the work of a genius. But if structured plots and well-defined characters is what you're looking for, then this may not be for you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12938921070997803586" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2282822026" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2282822026" />
    <a title="Bob Newman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72341080-bob-newman"><img alt="Bob Newman" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2282822026?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Newman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Newman" href="/user/show/72341080-bob-newman">Bob Newman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72341080-bob-newman?shelf=european-literature">european-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2282822026" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3022120566679806886">"Nerdy Wordsmith Rats On Flame, Conks Out Young"<br/><br/>Fidel Castro and his bearded men charged down out of the Sierra Maestra and paraded victorious through the streets of Havana to delirious cheers of adoring crowds. Mao Tsetung arrived with his vast armies at Beijing and declared that "China had stood up". The `Internationale' played and a brave new world began. We dreamed we would change the world as youths, we might die for a great cause, we yelled at barricades (of whatever material-or perhaps th</span>
  <span id="freeText3022120566679806886" style="display:none">"Nerdy Wordsmith Rats On Flame, Conks Out Young"<br /><br />Fidel Castro and his bearded men charged down out of the Sierra Maestra and paraded victorious through the streets of Havana to delirious cheers of adoring crowds. Mao Tsetung arrived with his vast armies at Beijing and declared that "China had stood up". The `Internationale' played and a brave new world began. We dreamed we would change the world as youths, we might die for a great cause, we yelled at barricades (of whatever material-or perhaps they were intangible) and loved with the passions of the times. Repression of anybody (except "the exploiters") never appeared on the cards, no, it was freedom in the air. Hasn't this atmosphere repeated itself time and time again, across the globe ? And there's always a poet or two to inscribe glorious verses on the stones of History. Byron, Mayakovsky, Rimbaud, Marti, Rizal. But what if `the Revolution' ushers in a period of less freedom, greater oppression, and wider stupidity that leads to mass fatalities ? Then what kind of poet would you need ? Well, what kind do you get ? Artists who paint girl + tractor. Novelists who write books called "Cement". And poets like Jaromil, the subject of this great novel. Fidel called the people who fled the new Cuba "gusanos" or worms. Reading Kundera's novel about Czechoslovakia, you feel strongly that the gusanos remained and cooperated, wrote poetry in praise of the unpraise-able. Or, maybe there's a global glut of gusanos. Maybe a gusano poet is about as necessary as wings on a turtle.<br /><br />OK, this novel is a fictional biography of a very weedy mama's boy who remains naïve, protected and innocent despite everything that happens around him, even the death of his father in a concentration camp. The world around the main characters, the society at large, remain pale and nearly invisible. He (and we) really see nobody except his mother---his loves are extensions of his ego, his poetry or paintings the same. Dreams and fantasy are his stock in trade, his alter-ego jumps in and out of beds, while Jaromil stews. All is self-absorption. In modern America, this poet would be called a "dweeb". We have to laugh at Jaromil or scorn him. LIFE IS ELSEWHERE is a satire that concentrates on unpleasant aspects of the human condition so well that you cringe time and time again. Kundera spares no one, not his main character and certainly not his readers. Jaromil is surrounded, as the author says, with a wall of mirrors, and cannot see beyond. We look into our own mirrors as we read. It's doubtful that we admire the reflections. The basic themes are human nature, art and literature in society, and the sad tribulations of a small nation. Kundera, like Brazil's Machado de Assis, cuts his books up into extremely small chapters, which is an effective tool in expert hands. Each one makes a point, introduces an irony, or engages in new soul-searching. The plot of LIFE IS ELSEWHERE is minor; it is the process of writing and thinking about the issues that counts. Ah, well, readers, I'm not giving anything away to say that the message here is that people who fail to live life to the fullest always pine for some far away paradise where great deeds would be accomplished effortlessly, and imagine that "life is elsewhere". These deluded ones are capable of the dirtiest deeds. Oh, yeah, this is a good book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3022120566679806886" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11886443335479382699">I absolutely loved this book, but it's probably not for everyone. <br/><br/>It's ostensibly about a young poet and his overbearing mother during the period following the Czech communist revolution, but that's basically just an excuse for Kundera to talk about art, poetry, politics, and integrity for four hundred pages. You know, all that human stuff that's a little uncomfortable to talk/read about unless it's done really well. Kundera's ideas are challenging and provocative, but his irrepressible charm ma</span>
  <span id="freeText11886443335479382699" style="display:none">I absolutely loved this book, but it's probably not for everyone. <br /><br />It's ostensibly about a young poet and his overbearing mother during the period following the Czech communist revolution, but that's basically just an excuse for Kundera to talk about art, poetry, politics, and integrity for four hundred pages. You know, all that human stuff that's a little uncomfortable to talk/read about unless it's done really well. Kundera's ideas are challenging and provocative, but his irrepressible charm makes you forget that you're engaging with serious discussion of BIG TOPICS. <br /><br />It's been maybe six years since I read "Laughter and Forgetting" and "The Unbearable Lightness of Being," but this book (a relatively recent English translation, from 2000, which I thought was interesting) makes me want to go back and read him all over again. It's way more incendiary and awesome than just about anything I've read from contemporary fiction writers.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14031644545562610913">A nice subtitle for this one might be "Portrait of the Artist as a Young Idiot" and it's probably still more relevant than you might think. It is the story of a young man, almost disturbingly attached to his mother and she to him, who tries to be a poet in the political turmoil of post-WW2 Czechoslovakia. It's more a meditation on the idiocy of the upstart and immature and the almost-guaranteed failure of naive idealism, which plays such a big part today in American culture, at least, though you</span>
  <span id="freeText14031644545562610913" style="display:none">A nice subtitle for this one might be "Portrait of the Artist as a Young Idiot" and it's probably still more relevant than you might think. It is the story of a young man, almost disturbingly attached to his mother and she to him, who tries to be a poet in the political turmoil of post-WW2 Czechoslovakia. It's more a meditation on the idiocy of the upstart and immature and the almost-guaranteed failure of naive idealism, which plays such a big part today in American culture, at least, though youth barely bothers with the arts these days.<br />As with all Kundera, it is gentle, funny, and sensually brutal.</span>

<div id="review_2844067370" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2844067370" />
    <a title="Artemis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18622356-artemis"><img alt="Artemis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591520208p2/18622356.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2844067370?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Artemis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Artemis" href="/user/show/18622356-artemis">Artemis</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2844067370" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6231976829288179064">I always find it hard to accurately appraise books whose protagonists I hate. Both Jaromil and his mother are small and odious, and my distaste is magnified by the fact that Jaromil is a character whom, if portrayed through a different narratorial lens, I likely would have loved. That’s the point of this book, though, a biting and deeply effective critique of the lyrical poet — and often Kundera seamlessly transitions into speaking of Hugo and Rimbaud and Pushkin in the same breath (and indeed l</span>
  <span id="freeText6231976829288179064" style="display:none">I always find it hard to accurately appraise books whose protagonists I hate. Both Jaromil and his mother are small and odious, and my distaste is magnified by the fact that Jaromil is a character whom, if portrayed through a different narratorial lens, I likely would have loved. That’s the point of this book, though, a biting and deeply effective critique of the lyrical poet — and often Kundera seamlessly transitions into speaking of Hugo and Rimbaud and Pushkin in the same breath (and indeed literally in the same sentence) as his descriptions of Jaromil. The reality probably lies somewhere in the middle of the more familiar exultations of poesy and this unsympathetic critique, although since the former are so commonplace the latter deserves appreciation. Kundera’s judgement of poetry as immaturity, and of the poet’s absolutism as the desperate wish to return back to the monism of the womb, is compelling. His deeply Freudian worldview is obviously compelling to me, but I feel that his characters are *too* Freudian, in the sense that their entire personality seems utterly determined by external (and stereotypically Freudian) sources (in a way that almost makes them caricatures). Which is not to accuse this book of being simplistic - far from that - but it is a qualm I have with the protagonists. <br /><br />Highlights include: <br />“The story of two people about to become lovers is so eternal that it almost made us forget History. How pleasant it is to recount such love stories! How sweet it would be to forget the monster that saps our brief lives as cement for its vain monuments. How sweet it would be to forget History!” <br /><br />“Maman had nothing but contempt for her brother-in-law, ever since she had heard him declare that Voltaire was a physicist who invented volts” LMAO<br /><br />“You think that just because it’s already happened, the past is finished and unchangeable? Oh no, the past is cloaked in a multicolored taffeta and every time we look at it we see a different hue.”<br /><br />Also! Apparently the scene in which Marius gets angry at Cosette because she accidentally exposed her ankle in public while holding her skirt is based on a real incidence between Hugo and his lover and he angrily rebuked her for showing her ankle in one of their letter exchanges .... LOL</span>
  <a data-text-id="6231976829288179064" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_594662467" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/594662467" />
    <a title="Blake" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9875910-blake"><img alt="Blake" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508355867p2/9875910.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/594662467?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blake" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blake" href="/user/show/9875910-blake">Blake</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9875910-blake?shelf=postmodern">postmodern</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer594662467" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2747736778802060278">For me this a really well written, awful book. I feel like Kundera wasted his considerable talents tackling the issues of adolescent male sex anxiety and wanting-to-be-an-adult-already and created the whiniest, most self-centered worm of a protagonist I've read. He's investigations into the nature and purpose of poetry and the life of a poet are good, and his descriptions of interior life are superb, but both are too few and far between to make up for the whole. Even if the Poet were likable in </span>
  <span id="freeText2747736778802060278" style="display:none">For me this a really well written, awful book. I feel like Kundera wasted his considerable talents tackling the issues of adolescent male sex anxiety and wanting-to-be-an-adult-already and created the whiniest, most self-centered worm of a protagonist I've read. He's investigations into the nature and purpose of poetry and the life of a poet are good, and his descriptions of interior life are superb, but both are too few and far between to make up for the whole. Even if the Poet were likable in some way, the awkward transition into adulthood from the prospective of a disgruntled, narcissistic beta-male is just lame storytelling. I should mention as a disclaimer that I dislike the entire genre, right down to Catcher in the Rye.<br /><br />That said, the relationship between the protag and his mother, or more specifically, their relationship as seen through her, was absurd enough to be gripping.<br /><br />It was also interesting to see a child of a concentration camp victim making a habit of drawing soldiers with animal faces 12 years before Art Spiegelman inked MAUS and whole alternative lifetimes spent in dreams within dreams within dreams 36 years before the movie Inception came out. The ideas that seem the most original are usually ones that were already out there. Being an artist has more to do with gestalt than original thought. Everyone you think is great is standing on the shoulders of giants.<br /><br />That said, I still think Kundera is a giant. I just think Life is Elsewhere is bilge-water compared to the fine wine of Unbearable Lightness of Being. Read that one instead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2747736778802060278" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_256221730" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/256221730" />
    <a title="Corey Pung" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7184884-corey-pung"><img alt="Corey Pung" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1375050390p2/7184884.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/256221730?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Corey Pung" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Corey Pung" href="/user/show/7184884-corey-pung">Corey Pung</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer256221730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7109165492791922962">Life is Elsewhere is Kundera’s brazen send-up of the world of poetry, particularly the world of poets who involve themselves with politics. It follows in the tradition of the nineteenth century novel where your given the main character’s life from birth onwards, although it does cut out portions, a la A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man. The main character is Jaromil, a man who has a painfully awkward childhood (complete with a few hysterically funny scenes) who grows up to believe he’s dest</span>
  <span id="freeText7109165492791922962" style="display:none">Life is Elsewhere is Kundera’s brazen send-up of the world of poetry, particularly the world of poets who involve themselves with politics. It follows in the tradition of the nineteenth century novel where your given the main character’s life from birth onwards, although it does cut out portions, a la A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man. The main character is Jaromil, a man who has a painfully awkward childhood (complete with a few hysterically funny scenes) who grows up to believe he’s destined to be a poet. This isn’t the sort of vaunted or glorified view of artistry that you get with Somerset Maugham; Jaromil does on occasion make himself out to be a martyr for his artistry, but what’s so scathingly delightful about this novel is how the author exposes the foolishness of this posturing. As the novel progresses, Jaromil becomes more and more of a pretentious and foppish artist while imagining himself to be a literary hero. Things take a dark turn when he involves himself and his writing in the political turmoil of his era.<br /><br />To read my full review, please go to: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://coreysbook.wordpress.com/2011/07/17/overlooked-book-life-is-elsewhere-by-milan-kundera/">http://coreysbook.wordpress.com/2011/...</a><br /><br />If you like books about the weird world of artists, you might like<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11724600.The_Madness_of_Art_Short_Stories" title="The Madness of Art Short Stories by Corey Pung" rel="nofollow noopener">The Madness of Art: Short Stories</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="7109165492791922962" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Dariana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5950260-dariana"><img alt="Dariana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505170603p2/5950260.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/193817723?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer193817723" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7917096739414170918">Milan Kundera sets the destiny of a whole generation under the sign of failure. <br/><br/><br/><br/>The hero isn't a young boy from those places and times, but one that lives his painful growth in the age when the communism settled down in Czechoslovakia. He is a vulnerable adolescent, haunted by the fear of pathetic, but he has an extreme purity. <br/><br/><br/><br/>The poet Jaromil is attracted by the ideology of Marx, which promises him a revenge against a world that can not include him. Step by step, he becomes a prisoner of a sys</span>
  <span id="freeText7917096739414170918" style="display:none">Milan Kundera sets the destiny of a whole generation under the sign of failure. <br /><br /><br /><br />The hero isn't a young boy from those places and times, but one that lives his painful growth in the age when the communism settled down in Czechoslovakia. He is a vulnerable adolescent, haunted by the fear of pathetic, but he has an extreme purity. <br /><br /><br /><br />The poet Jaromil is attracted by the ideology of Marx, which promises him a revenge against a world that can not include him. Step by step, he becomes a prisoner of a system, that requests him to abandon everything he worshiped and to enter a flatness, that claims to be the only true literature. <br /><br /><br /><br />His purity becomes an abjection, when he denounces his girlfriend to the police. The poet's fall, compared with the falls of so many great poets of the world, is very abrupt and speaks about everybody's fall that got caught in the system.<br /><br /><br /><br />This is why Kundera's novel couldn't be published in 1973, only outside of the Czechoslovakia's borders.</span>
